<th scope="row" style="padding-right: 1em">Other work</th>
<td>Served as military advisor to the <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West German</a> government</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Fritz Erich Georg Eduard von Lewinski</b>, known as <b>Erich von Manstein</b> (24 November 1887 – 9 June 1973), was a German commander of the <i><a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>'s armed forces during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>. He attained the rank of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_Marshal_(Germany)" title="Field Marshal (Germany)">field marshal</a>.</p>
<p>Born into an aristocratic <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> family with a long history of military service, Manstein joined the army at a young age and saw service on <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Eastern Front (World War I)">Eastern Front</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a> (1914–18). He rose to the rank of captain by the end of the war and was active in the inter-war period helping Germany rebuild her armed forces. In September 1939, during the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a> at the beginning of the Second World War, he was serving as Chief of Staff to <a href="/wiki/Gerd_von_Rundstedt" title="Gerd von Rundstedt">Gerd von Rundstedt</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_South" title="Army Group South">Army Group South</a>. <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> chose Manstein's strategy for the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">invasion of France</a> of May 1940, a plan later refined by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Halder" title="Franz Halder">Franz Halder</a> and other members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OKH" title="OKH">OKH</a>. Anticipating a firm Allied reaction should the main thrust of the invasion take place through the Netherlands, Manstein devised an innovative tactic—later known as the <i>Sichelschnitt</i> ("sickle cut")—that called for an attack through the woods of the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes" title="Ardennes">Ardennes</a> and a rapid drive to the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, thus cutting off the French and Allied armies in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>. Attaining the rank of <a href="/wiki/General_(Germany)" title="General (Germany)">general</a> at the end of the campaign, he was active in the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">invasion of the Soviet Union</a> in June 1941 and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sevastopol_(1941%E2%80%931942)" title="Siege of Sevastopol (1941–1942)">Siege of Sevastopol (1941–1942)</a>, and was promoted to field marshal on 1 July 1942. He also participated in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Leningrad" title="Siege of Leningrad">Siege of Leningrad</a>.</p>
<p>Germany's fortunes in the war began to take an unfavourable turn later in 1942, especially in the catastrophic <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad" title="Battle of Stalingrad">Battle of Stalingrad</a>, where Manstein commanded a failed relief effort ("<a href="/wiki/Operation_Winter_Storm" title="Operation Winter Storm">Operation Winter Storm</a>") in December. Later known as the "backhand blow", Manstein's counteroffensive in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Kharkov" title="Third Battle of Kharkov">Third Battle of Kharkov</a> (February–March 1943) regained substantial territory and resulted in the destruction of three Soviet armies and the retreat of three others. He was one of the primary commanders at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kursk" title="Battle of Kursk">Battle of Kursk</a> (July–August 1943), one of the largest tank battles in history. His ongoing disagreements with Hitler over the conduct of the war led to his dismissal in March 1944. He never obtained another command and was taken prisoner by the British in August 1945, several months after Germany's defeat.</p>
<p>Manstein gave testimony at the main <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a> of war criminals in August 1946, and prepared a paper that, along with his later memoirs, helped cultivate the myth of a "<a href="/wiki/Clean_Wehrmacht" title="Clean Wehrmacht">clean Wehrmacht</a>"—the myth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010432.E2.80.93434_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010432.E2.80.93434-1">[1]</a></sup> that the German armed forces were not culpable for the atrocities of <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>. In 1949 he <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Erich_von_Manstein" title="Trial of Erich von Manstein">was tried</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> for war crimes and was convicted on nine of seventeen counts, including the poor treatment of prisoners of war and failing to protect civilian lives in his sphere of operations. His sentence of eighteen years in prison was later reduced to twelve, and he served only four years before being released in 1953. As a military advisor to the <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West German</a> government in the mid-1950s, he helped re-establish the armed forces. His successful memoir, <i><a href="/wiki/Verlorene_Siege" title="Verlorene Siege">Verlorene Siege</a></i> (1955), translated into English as <i>Lost Victories</i>, was highly critical of Hitler's leadership, and only dealt with the military aspects of the war, ignoring its political and ethical contexts. Manstein died in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> in 1973.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Manstein was born <b>Fritz Erich Georg Eduard von Lewinski</b> in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, the tenth son of a <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> aristocrat and <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> general, <a href="/wiki/Eduard_von_Lewinski" title="Eduard von Lewinski">Eduard von Lewinski</a> (1829–1906), and Helene von Sperling (1847–1910). His father's family had <a href="/wiki/Kashubians" title="Kashubians">Kashubian</a> ancestry and was entitled to use the <a href="/wiki/Brochwicz_coat_of_arms" title="Brochwicz coat of arms">Brochwicz coat of arms</a> (Brochwicz III).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKosk2001.5B.5BCategory:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2012.5D.5D.3Csup_class.3D.22noprint_Inline-Template_.22_style.3D.22white-space:nowrap.3B.22.3E.5B.3Ci.3E.5B.5BWikipedia:Citing_sources.7C.3Cspan_title.3D.22This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears..7F.27.22.60UNIQ--nowiki-00000005-QINU.60.22.27.7F_.28October_2012.29.22.3Epage.C2.A0needed.3C.2Fspan.3E.5D.5D.3C.2Fi.3E.5D.3C.2Fsup.3E_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKosk2001.5B.5BCategory:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_October_2012.5D.5D.3Csup_class.3D.22noprint_Inline-Template_.22_style.3D.22white-space:nowrap.3B.22.3E.5B.3Ci.3E.5B.5BWikipedia:Citing_sources.7C.3Cspan_title.3D.22This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears..7F.27.22.60UNIQ--nowiki-00000005-QINU.60.22.27.7F_.28October_2012.29.22.3Epage.C2.A0needed.3C.2Fspan.3E.5D.5D.3C.2Fi.3E.5D.3C.2Fsup.3E-2">[2]</a></sup> Hedwig von Sperling (1852–1925), Helene's younger sister, was married to Lieutenant General Georg von Manstein (1844–1913); the couple was unable to have children, so they adopted Erich. They had previously adopted Erich's cousin Martha, the daughter of Helene's and Hedwig's deceased brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201010_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201010-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein's biological and adoptive fathers were both Prussian generals, as were his mother's brother and both his grandfathers (one of them, <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_Gustav_von_Manstein" title="Albrecht Gustav von Manstein">Albrecht Gustav von Manstein</a>, had led a corps in the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a> of 1870–71). Sixteen relatives on each side of his family were military officers, many of whom rose to the rank of general. <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a>, the future <i>Generalfeldmarschall</i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_Germany_(1919%E2%80%931945)" title="President of Germany (1919–1945)">President of Germany</a>, was his uncle; Hindenburg's wife, Gertrud, was the sister of Hedwig and Helene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201012_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201012-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein attended the Imperial <i>Lyzeum</i>, a Catholic <i><a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">Gymnasium</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, from 1894 to 1899.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201013_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201013-5">[5]</a></sup> In March 1906, after six years in the cadet corps in <a href="/wiki/Pl%C3%B6n" title="Plön">Plön</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lichterfelde_(Berlin)" title="Lichterfelde (Berlin)">Groß-Lichterfelde</a>, he was commissioned into the Third Foot Guards Regiment (<i>Garde zu Fuß</i>) as an ensign. He was promoted to lieutenant in January 1907 and in October 1913 began the three-year officer training programme at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prussian_War_Academy" title="Prussian War Academy">Prussian War Academy</a>. However, Manstein only completed the first year of the programme, as when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a> began in August 1914 all students of the Academy were ordered to report for active service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201020.E2.80.9321.2C_23_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201020.E2.80.9321.2C_23-6">[6]</a></sup> He never completed the remainder of his general staff officer training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201014_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201014-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_military_career">Early military career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early military career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_World_War">First World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the First World War, Manstein served on both the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Eastern Front (World War I)">Eastern</a> Fronts. At the beginning of the war he was promoted to lieutenant and participated in the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> with the 2nd Guard Reserve Infantry Regiment. In August 1914 he took part in the capture of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namur_(city)" title="Namur (city)">Namur</a>, the site of a massive citadel surrounded by outlying forts. In September, Manstein's unit was one of two transferred to <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a> and attached to the <a href="/wiki/8th_Army_(German_Empire)" title="8th Army (German Empire)">Eighth Army</a>, commanded by Hindenburg. After seeing action in the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_the_Masurian_Lakes" title="First Battle of the Masurian Lakes">First Battle of the Masurian Lakes</a>, his unit was soon reassigned to the <a href="/wiki/9th_Army_(German_Empire)" title="9th Army (German Empire)">Ninth Army</a>, which was in the process of advancing from <a href="/wiki/Upper_Silesia" title="Upper Silesia">Upper Silesia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>. Overstretched, the Ninth Army was forced to withdraw in the face of a Russian counterattack, and on 16 November Manstein was wounded during the retreat when he was among a detachment that stormed a Russian entrenchment. He was shot in the left shoulder and the left knee; one bullet hit his <a href="/wiki/Sciatic_nerve" title="Sciatic nerve">sciatic nerve</a>, causing the leg to be numb. Recovery took six months in hospital in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beuthen" title="Beuthen">Beuthen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wiesbaden" title="Wiesbaden">Wiesbaden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201014.E2.80.9315_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201014.E2.80.9315-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201023.E2.80.9327_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201023.E2.80.9327-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000178_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000178-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a period of home leave, on 17 June 1915 Manstein was reassigned as assistant general staff officer of operations to the <a href="/wiki/10th_Army_(German_Empire)" title="10th Army (German Empire)">Tenth Army</a>, commanded by <a href="/wiki/Max_von_Gallwitz" title="Max von Gallwitz">Max von Gallwitz</a>. Soon promoted to captain, he learned first-hand how to plan and conduct offensive operations as the Tenth Army undertook successful attacks on Poland, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>. During offensive operations at <a href="/wiki/Verdun" title="Verdun">Verdun</a> in early 1916, Manstein was stationed with Gallwitz and his staff at a new headquarters near the action. He next served as a staff supply officer under General <a href="/wiki/Fritz_von_Below" title="Fritz von Below">Fritz von Below</a> and chief of staff <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fritz_von_Lossberg" title="Fritz von Lossberg">Fritz von Lossberg</a> at a command post near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Somme" title="River Somme">River Somme</a>; the area was the scene of several battles over the course of the war. British and French operations from July to November 1916 forced a German withdrawal over the winter to the <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_Line" title="Hindenburg Line">Hindenburg Line</a>, a series of defensive positions between Verdun and <a href="/wiki/Lens,_Pas-de-Calais" title="Lens, Pas-de-Calais">Lens</a>. Manstein continued to serve under Below until October 1917, when he was transferred as chief of staff to the <a href="/wiki/4th_Cavalry_Division_(German_Empire)" title="4th Cavalry Division (German Empire)">4th Cavalry Division</a>, serving in <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a> during the German occupation of the area. As a result of the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk" title="Treaty of Brest-Litovsk">Treaty of Brest-Litovsk</a> in March 1918, Manstein's unit was no longer needed on the Eastern Front; he was reassigned to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=213th_Infantry_Division_(German_Empire)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="213th Infantry Division (German Empire) (page does not exist)">213th Infantry Division</a> near <a href="/wiki/Reims" title="Reims">Reims</a>. The German Imperial Army saw some minor successes in the area but was beginning to lose the war. The <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_11_November_1918" title="Armistice of 11 November 1918">armistice</a> was signed on 11 November 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201027.E2.80.9331_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201027.E2.80.9331-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inter-war_era">Inter-war era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Inter-war era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Manstein married Jutta Sibylle von Loesch, the daughter of a <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesian</a> landowner, in 1920. He proposed marriage after having known her for only three days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000180_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000180-12">[12]</a></sup> She died in 1966. They had three children: a daughter, Gisela (born 1921), and two sons, Gero (born 1922) and Rüdiger (born 1929).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201020_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201020-13">[13]</a></sup> Gero died on the battlefield in the northern sector of the Eastern Front on 29 October 1942 while serving as a lieutenant in the <i>Wehrmacht</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk20107.E2.80.938.3B_28_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk20107.E2.80.938.3B_28-14">[14]</a></sup> Gisela was married to Major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edel-Heinrich_Zachariae-Lingenthal" title="Edel-Heinrich Zachariae-Lingenthal">Edel-Heinrich Zachariae-Lingenthal</a>, a highly decorated officer who commanded II. Panzer-Regiment 15 during the Second World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000197_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000197-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein remained in the armed forces after the war ended. In 1918 he volunteered for a staff position with the Frontier Defence Force in Breslau (now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wroclaw" title="Wroclaw">Wroclaw</a>) and served there until 1919.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201038_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201038-16">[16]</a></sup> As part of <i>Gruppenkommando</i> II, he participated in the restructuring of the German Imperial Army of 500,000 men into the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichswehr" title="Reichswehr">Reichswehr</a></i>, the army of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a> (restricted to 100,000 men by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Versailles_Treaty" title="Versailles Treaty">Versailles Treaty</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201040_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201040-17">[17]</a></sup> Recognised from an early age as a talented and intelligent commander, Manstein was chosen as one of only 4,000 officers permitted under the treaty. In 1921 he was appointed company commander of the sixth company of the 5th Prussian Infantry Regiment and next served as a staff officer for <i>Wehrkreiskommando</i> II and IV, teaching military history and tactics until 1927. That year he was promoted to major and served with the <a href="/wiki/German_General_Staff" title="German General Staff">General Staff</a> at the <i>Reichswehr</i> Ministry in Berlin, visiting other countries to learn about their military facilities and helping to draft mobilisation plans for the army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201021.E2.80.9322.2C_26_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201021.E2.80.9322.2C_26-18">[18]</a></sup> Promoted to lieutenant colonel, he was given command of the light infantry battalion of the 4th Infantry Regiment, serving with that unit until 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201059.2C_64_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201059.2C_64-19">[19]</a></sup> In 1933 the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machtergreifung" title="Machtergreifung">seized power</a> in Germany, thus ending the Weimar period. In violation of the Versailles treaty, the <i>Reichswehr</i> had been secretly re-arming since the 1920s; the new government formally renounced the Treaty and proceeded with large scale rearmament and expansion of the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201023_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201023-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk20107.E2.80.939_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk20107.E2.80.939-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein was moved back to Berlin as full colonel in February 1934, serving as chief of staff of <i>Wehrkreiskommando</i> III.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201064_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201064-22">[22]</a></sup> On 1 July 1935 he was appointed the Head of the Operations Branch of the Army General Staff (<i>Generalstab des Heeres</i>), part of the Army High Command (<i><a href="/wiki/Oberkommando_des_Heeres" title="Oberkommando des Heeres">Oberkommando des Heeres</a></i> – OKH).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201073_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201073-23">[23]</a></sup> During his tenure there, Manstein was one of the people responsible for the development of <i><a href="/wiki/Fall_Rot" title="Fall Rot">Fall Rot</a></i> (Case Red), a defensive plan to protect Germany from attack by France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201051_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201051-24">[24]</a></sup> During this period Manstein came into contact with <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Guderian" title="Heinz Guderian">Heinz Guderian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Lutz" title="Oswald Lutz">Oswald Lutz</a>, who advocated drastic changes in warfare, emphasising the role of the <a href="/wiki/Panzer" title="Panzer">Panzer</a>. However, officers like <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Beck" title="Ludwig Beck">Ludwig Beck</a>, Chief of the Army General Staff, were against such drastic changes, and therefore Manstein proposed an alternative: the development of <i><a href="/wiki/Sturmgesch%C3%BCtz" title="Sturmgeschütz">Sturmgeschütze</a></i> (StuG), self-propelled assault guns that would provide heavy direct-fire support to infantry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin201079.E2.80.9382_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin201079.E2.80.9382-25">[25]</a></sup> In World War II, the resulting StuG vehicles proved to be among the most successful and cost-effective German weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk20109_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk20109-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was promoted to major general in October 1936, becoming the Deputy Chief of Staff (<i>Oberquartiermeister I</i>) to General Beck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201043_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201043-27">[27]</a></sup> On 4 February 1938 Manstein was transferred to the command of the <a href="/wiki/18th_Infantry_Division_(Wehrmacht)" title="18th Infantry Division (Wehrmacht)">18th Infantry Division</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liegnitz" title="Liegnitz">Liegnitz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>, with the rank of <a href="/wiki/General_(Germany)" title="General (Germany)">Generalleutnant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010100_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010100-28">[28]</a></sup> The transfer meant that Manstein did not receive Beck's post as Chief of the Army General Staff that August (Beck had resigned, in spite of Manstein's urgings not to do so, as he felt Hitler's intended invasion of <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> that October would be premature). The position was given to General <a href="/wiki/Franz_Halder" title="Franz Halder">Franz Halder</a>, who had taken Manstein's former post as Deputy Chief of Staff. The incident created a lasting hatred between the two men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201056.E2.80.9357.2C_62.E2.80.9363_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201056.E2.80.9357.2C_62.E2.80.9363-29">[29]</a></sup> On 20 April 1939 Manstein delivered a speech at the celebration of Hitler's 50th birthday, in which he praised Hitler as a leader sent by God to save Germany. He warned the "hostile world" that if it kept erecting "ramparts around Germany to block the way of the German people towards their future", then he would be quite happy to see the world plunged into another world war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200897_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200897-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKopp2003471.E2.80.93534.2C_512_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKopp2003471.E2.80.93534.2C_512-31">[31]</a></sup> Israeli historian <a href="/wiki/Omer_Bartov" title="Omer Bartov">Omer Bartov</a> notes that the rise of officers such as Manstein was part of a tendency for technocratic officers, usually ardent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Socialist" title="National Socialist">National Socialists</a>, to come to the fore; his opinion is that the Wehrmacht was fully integrated into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Reich" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a>, not a separate apolitical organisation independent of the Nazi regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartov1999145_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartov1999145-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland_(1939)" title="Invasion of Poland (1939)">Invasion of Poland (1939)</a></div>
<p>On 18 August 1939, in preparation for <a href="/wiki/Fall_Weiss_(1939)" title="Fall Weiss (1939)"><i>Fall Weiss</i></a> (Case White)—the German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland_(1939)" title="Invasion of Poland (1939)">invasion of Poland</a>—Manstein was appointed Chief of Staff to <a href="/wiki/Gerd_von_Rundstedt" title="Gerd von Rundstedt">Gerd von Rundstedt</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_South" title="Army Group South">Army Group South</a>. Here he worked along with Rundstedt's Chief of Operations, Colonel <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnther_Blumentritt" title="Günther Blumentritt">Günther Blumentritt</a>, to develop the operational plan. Rundstedt accepted Manstein's plan calling for the concentration of the majority of the army group's armoured units into <a href="/wiki/Walther_von_Reichenau" title="Walther von Reichenau">Walther von Reichenau</a>'s <a href="/wiki/10th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="10th Army (Wehrmacht)">10th Army</a>, with the objective of a decisive breakthrough which would lead to the encirclement of Polish forces west of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Vistula" title="River Vistula">River Vistula</a>. In Manstein's plan, two other armies comprising Army Group South, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_List" title="Wilhelm List">Wilhelm List</a>'s <a href="/wiki/14th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="14th Army (Wehrmacht)">14th Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Blaskowitz" title="Johannes Blaskowitz">Johannes Blaskowitz</a>'s <a href="/wiki/8th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="8th Army (Wehrmacht)">8th Army</a>, would provide flank support for Reichenau's armoured thrust towards <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, the Polish capital. Privately, Manstein was lukewarm about the Polish campaign, thinking that it would be better to keep Poland as a buffer between Germany and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. He also worried about an Allied attack from the west once the Polish campaign was underway, which would draw Germany into a two-front war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein took part in a conference on 22 August 1939 where Hitler underlined to his commanders the need for the physical destruction of Poland as a nation. After the war, he would state in his memoirs that he did not recognise at the time of this meeting that Hitler was going to pursue a policy of extermination against the Poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010117_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010117-34">[34]</a></sup> He did become aware of the policy later on, as he and other <i>Wehrmacht</i> generals received reports<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELongerich.2C_Chapter_102003_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELongerich.2C_Chapter_102003-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201081.E2.80.9388_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201081.E2.80.9388-36">[36]</a></sup> on the activities of the <i><a href="/wiki/Einsatzgruppen" title="Einsatzgruppen">Einsatzgruppen</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">Schutzstaffel</a></i> (SS) death squads tasked with following the army into Poland to kill intellectuals and other civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200814.E2.80.9315_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200814.E2.80.9315-37">[37]</a></sup> These squads were also assigned to round up Jews and others for relocation to <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">ghettos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a>. Manstein later faced three charges of war crimes relating to Jewish and civilian deaths in the sectors under his control, and the mistreatment and deaths of prisoners of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010469.E2.80.93470_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010469.E2.80.93470-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Launched on 1 September 1939, the invasion began successfully. In Army Group South's area of responsibility under Rundstedt, the 8th, 10th and 14th Armies pursued the retreating Poles. The initial plan was for the 8th Army, the northernmost of the three, to advance towards <a href="/wiki/%C5%81%C3%B3d%C5%BA" title="Łódź">Łódź</a>. The 10th Army, with its motorised divisions, was to move quickly towards the Vistula, and the 14th Army was to advance and attempt to encircle the Polish troops in the <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> area. These actions led to the encirclement and defeat of Polish forces in the <a href="/wiki/Radom" title="Radom">Radom</a> area on 8–14 September by six German corps. Meanwhile, the German Eighth Army was under attack from the north, so elements of the Fourth, Eighth and Tenth Armies were quickly redeployed with air support in an improvised attempt to cut off any Polish break-out back towards Warsaw. The flexibility and agility of the German forces led to the defeat of nine Polish infantry divisions and other units in the resulting <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bzura" title="Battle of the Bzura">Battle of the Bzura</a> (8–19 September), the largest engagement of the war thus far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010120.E2.80.93125_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010120.E2.80.93125-39">[39]</a></sup> The conquest of Poland was quickly over, with the last Polish military units surrendering on 6 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans20087_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans20087-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="687" data-file-width="890" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG/220px-Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG/330px-Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG/440px-Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Plan_Yellow.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The evolution of German plans for <i>Fall Gelb</i>, the invasion of the Low Countries. The series begins at the left upper corner.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Fall Gelb</i> ("Case Yellow"), the initial plan for the invasion of France, was prepared by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commander-in-Chief" title="Commander-in-Chief">Commander-in-Chief</a> of the Army <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonel_General" title="Colonel General">Colonel General</a> (<i>Generaloberst</i>) <a href="/wiki/Walther_von_Brauchitsch" title="Walther von Brauchitsch">Walther von Brauchitsch</a>, Halder, and other members of the OKH in early October 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201096_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201096-41">[41]</a></sup> Like the <a href="/wiki/Schlieffen_Plan" title="Schlieffen Plan">Schlieffen Plan</a> of World War I, it called for an encirclement attack through the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010140_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010140-42">[42]</a></sup> Hitler was not satisfied, so revisions of the plan continued throughout October. Manstein was not satisfied with the plan either, as it focused heavily on the northern wing; he felt an attack from this direction would lack the element of surprise and would expose the German forces to counterattacks from the south. The terrain in Belgium was not well-suited as a base of operations for further attacks on France, so Manstein felt the operation would fail to wipe out the enemy—as it did in the First World War—leading to only partial success and trench warfare. By the end of October Manstein had prepared the outline of a different plan and submitted it to the OKH via his superior, Rundstedt, for whom he was now serving as chief of staff of <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_A" title="Army Group A">Army Group A</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201098.E2.80.93102_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201098.E2.80.93102-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011.E2.80.9314_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011.E2.80.9314-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein's plan, developed with the informal cooperation of <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Guderian" title="Heinz Guderian">Heinz Guderian</a>, suggested that the Panzer divisions attack through the wooded hills of the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes" title="Ardennes">Ardennes</a> where no one would expect them, then establish bridgeheads on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Meuse" title="River Meuse">River Meuse</a> and rapidly drive to the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>. The Wehrmacht would thus cut off the French and <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied armies</a> in Belgium and <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>. This part of the plan later became known as the <i>Sichelschnitt</i> ("sickle cut"). Manstein's proposal also included a second thrust outflanking the <a href="/wiki/Maginot_Line" title="Maginot Line">Maginot Line</a>, which would allow the Wehrmacht to force any future defensive line much further south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011.E2.80.9314_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011.E2.80.9314-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010145_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010145-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The OKH originally rejected the proposal; Halder in particular said the plan was entirely without merit. However, on 11 November, Hitler ordered the reallocation of the forces needed to make a surprise thrust in <a href="/wiki/Sedan,_Ardennes" title="Sedan, Ardennes">Sedan</a>, thus pushing the plan in the direction that Manstein had suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010110.E2.80.93111_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010110.E2.80.93111-46">[46]</a></sup> When documents outlining details of <i>Fall Gelb</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechelen_Incident" title="Mechelen Incident">fell into the hands of the Belgians</a> on 10 January 1940, Hitler became even more receptive to change. But Manstein's superiors, Generals Halder and Brauchitsch, resented Manstein's repeated insistence that his plan be implemented in place of theirs. Halder had Manstein removed from Rundstedt's headquarters and sent to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stettin" title="Stettin">Stettin</a> to command the <a href="/wiki/XXXVIII_Army_Corps_(Wehrmacht)" title="XXXVIII Army Corps (Wehrmacht)">XXXVIII Army Corps</a> on 27 January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010117.E2.80.93119_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010117.E2.80.93119-47">[47]</a></sup> Hitler, still looking for a more aggressive plan, approved a modified version of Manstein's ideas, today known as the <a href="/wiki/Manstein_Plan" title="Manstein Plan">Manstein Plan</a>, after meeting with him on 17 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010132_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010132-48">[48]</a></sup> Manstein and his corps played a minor role during the operations in France, serving under <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnther_von_Kluge" title="Günther von Kluge">Günther von Kluge</a>'s <a href="/wiki/4th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="4th Army (Wehrmacht)">4th Army</a>. His corps helped achieve the first breakthrough east of <a href="/wiki/Amiens" title="Amiens">Amiens</a> during <i><a href="/wiki/Fall_Rot" title="Fall Rot">Fall Rot</a></i> ("Case Red" – the second phase of the invasion plan), and was the first to reach and cross the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Seine" title="River Seine">River Seine</a>. The invasion of France was an outstanding military success; Manstein was promoted to full <a href="/wiki/General_(Germany)" title="General (Germany)">general</a> and awarded the <a href="/wiki/Knight%27s_Cross_of_the_Iron_Cross" title="Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross">Knight's Cross of the Iron Cross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011.E2.80.9314_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201011.E2.80.9314-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010178.E2.80.93179_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010178.E2.80.93179-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain">Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Seel%C3%B6we" title="Operation Seelöwe">Operation Seelöwe</a></div>
<p>Manstein was a proponent of the prospective German invasion of Great Britain, named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Seel%C3%B6we" title="Operation Seelöwe">Operation Seelöwe</a>. He considered the operation risky but necessary. Early studies by various staff officers determined that air superiority was a prerequisite to the planned invasion. His corps was to be shipped across the English Channel from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boulogne" title="Boulogne">Boulogne</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bexhill-on-Sea" title="Bexhill-on-Sea">Bexhill</a> as one of four units assigned to the first wave. But as the Luftwaffe failed to overcome the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>, Operation Seelöwe was postponed indefinitely on 12 October. For the rest of the year, Manstein, with little to do, spent time in Paris and at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201016_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201016-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010186.2C_193_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010186.2C_193-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_Union">Soviet Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Soviet Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12,_Russland-Nord,_Erich_von_Manstein,_Brandenberger.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="508" data-file-width="800" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12%2C_Russland-Nord%2C_Erich_von_Manstein%2C_Brandenberger.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12%2C_Russland-Nord%2C_Erich_von_Manstein%2C_Brandenberger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12%2C_Russland-Nord%2C_Erich_von_Manstein%2C_Brandenberger.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12%2C_Russland-Nord%2C_Erich_von_Manstein%2C_Brandenberger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12%2C_Russland-Nord%2C_Erich_von_Manstein%2C_Brandenberger.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12%2C_Russland-Nord%2C_Erich_von_Manstein%2C_Brandenberger.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-209-0086-12,_Russland-Nord,_Erich_von_Manstein,_Brandenberger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Manstein with <i><a href="/wiki/General_der_Panzertruppe" title="General der Panzertruppe">General der Panzertruppe</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Erich_Brandenberger" title="Erich Brandenberger">Erich Brandenberger</a>, one of his divisional commanders, in June 1941</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In July 1940 the German High Command commenced planning <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>, the invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008160_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008160-52">[52]</a></sup> On 15 March 1941 Manstein was appointed commander of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LVI_Panzer_Corps_(Germany)" title="LVI Panzer Corps (Germany)">LVI Panzer Corps</a>; he was one of 250 commanders to be briefed for the upcoming major offensive, first seeing detailed plans of the offensive in May. His corps was part of the <a href="/wiki/4th_Panzer_Army" title="4th Panzer Army">Fourth Panzer Group</a> under the command of General <a href="/wiki/Erich_Hoepner" title="Erich Hoepner">Erich Hoepner</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Ritter_von_Leeb" title="Wilhelm Ritter von Leeb">Wilhelm Ritter von Leeb</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_North" title="Army Group North">Army Group North</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010198.E2.80.93199_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010198.E2.80.93199-53">[53]</a></sup> The Army Group was tasked with thrusting through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltic_States" title="Baltic States">Baltic States</a> and then advancing on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leningrad" title="Leningrad">Leningrad</a>. Manstein arrived at the front only six days prior to the launch of the offensive. Operation Barbarossa commenced on 22 June 1941 with a massive German attack along the whole front line. Manstein's corps was to advance with <a href="/wiki/Georg-Hans_Reinhardt" title="Georg-Hans Reinhardt">Georg-Hans Reinhardt</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/XLI_Panzer_Corps" title="XLI Panzer Corps">XLI Panzer Corps</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daugava_River" title="Daugava River">Dvina River</a> to secure the bridges near the town of <a href="/wiki/Daugavpils" title="Daugavpils">Daugavpils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010205_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010205-54">[54]</a></sup> The Soviet forces mounted a number of counterattacks, but those were aimed against Reinhardt's corps, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Raseiniai" title="Battle of Raseiniai">Battle of Raseiniai</a>. Manstein's corps advanced rapidly, reaching the Dvina River, 315 kilometres (196 mi) distant, in just 100 hours. Overextended and well ahead of the rest of the army group, he fended off a number of determined Soviet counterattacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010209.E2.80.93210_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010209.E2.80.93210-55">[55]</a></sup> After Reinhardt's corps closed in, the two corps were tasked with encircling the Soviet formations around <a href="/wiki/Luga,_Leningrad_Oblast" title="Luga, Leningrad Oblast">Luga</a> in a pincer movement.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_advance_into_USSR.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="272" data-file-width="354" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/German_advance_into_USSR.png/220px-German_advance_into_USSR.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/German_advance_into_USSR.png/330px-German_advance_into_USSR.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/German_advance_into_USSR.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_advance_into_USSR.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Progress of Army Group North, June to December 1941</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Again having penetrated deep into the Soviet lines with unprotected flanks, his corps was the target of a Soviet counteroffensive from 15 July at <a href="/wiki/Soltsy" title="Soltsy">Soltsy</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_11th_Army" title="Soviet 11th Army">Soviet 11th Army</a>, commanded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolai_Fyodorovich_Vatutin" title="Nikolai Fyodorovich Vatutin">Nikolai Vatutin</a>. Manstein's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8th_Panzer_Division" title="8th Panzer Division">8th Panzer Division</a> was cut off. Although it was able to fight its way free, it was badly mauled, and the Red Army succeeded in halting Manstein's advance at Luga. The corps regrouped at <a href="/wiki/Dno" title="Dno">Dno</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010217.E2.80.93218_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010217.E2.80.93218-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201016.E2.80.9320_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201016.E2.80.9320-57">[57]</a></sup> The 8th Panzers were sent on anti-partisan duties and Manstein was given the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4th_SS_Polizei_Division" title="4th SS Polizei Division">4th SS Polizei Division</a>. The attack on Luga was repeatedly delayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010220.E2.80.93221_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010220.E2.80.93221-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The assault on Luga was still underway when Manstein received orders on 10 August that his next task would be to begin the advance toward Leningrad. No sooner had he moved to his new headquarters at Lake Samro than he was told to send his men towards <a href="/wiki/Staraya_Russa" title="Staraya Russa">Staraya Russa</a> to relieve the X Corps, which was in danger of being encircled. On 12 August the Red Army had launched an offensive with the 11th and <a href="/wiki/34th_Army_(Soviet_Union)" title="34th Army (Soviet Union)">34th</a> Armies against Army Group North, cutting off three divisions. Frustrated with the loss of the 8th Panzer and the missed opportunity to advance on Leningrad, Manstein returned to Dno. His counteroffensive led to a major Soviet defeat when his unit encircled five Soviet divisions, receiving air support for the first time on that front. They captured 12,000 prisoners and 141 tanks. His opponent, General Kuzma M. Kachanov of the 34th Army, was subsequently court martialed and executed for the defeat. Manstein tried to obtain rest days for his men, who had been constantly fighting in poor terrain and increasingly poor weather since the start of the campaign, but to no avail. They were ordered to advance to the east on <a href="/wiki/Demyansk" title="Demyansk">Demyansk</a>. On 12 September, when he was near the city, he was informed that he would take over <a href="/wiki/11th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="11th Army (Wehrmacht)">11th Army</a> of <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_South" title="Army Group South">Army Group South</a> in Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201016.E2.80.9320_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201016.E2.80.9320-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010221.E2.80.93224_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010221.E2.80.93224-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crimea_and_the_Battle_of_Sevastopol">Crimea and the Battle of Sevastopol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Crimea and the Battle of Sevastopol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sevastopol_(1941%E2%80%931942)" title="Siege of Sevastopol (1941–1942)">Siege of Sevastopol (1941–1942)</a></div>
<p>In September 1941 Manstein was appointed commander of the 11th Army after its previous commander, Colonel-General <a href="/wiki/Eugen_Ritter_von_Schobert" title="Eugen Ritter von Schobert">Eugen Ritter von Schobert</a>, perished when his plane landed in a Soviet minefield. The 11th Army was tasked with invading the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_Peninsula" title="Crimean Peninsula">Crimean Peninsula</a>, capturing <a href="/wiki/Sevastopol" title="Sevastopol">Sevastopol</a>, and pursuing enemy forces on the flank of Army Group South during its advance into the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201020_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201020-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010227_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010227-61">[61]</a></sup> Hitler's intention was to prevent the Red Army from using airbases there, and to cut off the Soviet supply of oil from the Caucasus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010229_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010229-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein's forces—mostly infantry—achieved a rapid breakthrough during the first days against heavy Soviet resistance. After most of the neck of the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Perekop" title="Isthmus of Perekop">Perekop Isthmus</a> had been taken, his forces were substantially reduced, leaving six German divisions and the <a href="/wiki/Third_Army_(Romania)" title="Third Army (Romania)">Romanian Third Army</a>. The rest of the Perekop Isthmus was captured slowly and with some difficulty; Manstein complained of a lack of air support to contest Soviet air superiority in the region. He next created a mobile reconnaissance unit to press down the peninsula, cutting the road between <a href="/wiki/Simferopol" title="Simferopol">Simferopol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sevastopol" title="Sevastopol">Sevastopol</a> on 31 October. Simferopol was captured the next day. The 11th Army had captured all of the Crimean Peninsula—except for Sevastopol—by 16 November. Meanwhile, the Red Army had evacuated 300,000 personnel out of the city by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201021_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201021-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010233.E2.80.93235.2C_237_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010233.E2.80.93235.2C_237-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein's first attack on Sevastopol in November failed, and with insufficient forces left for an immediate assault, he ordered an <a href="/wiki/Investment_(military)" title="Investment (military)">investment</a> of the heavily fortified city. By 17 December he launched another offensive, which also failed. On 26 December the Soviet troops landed on the <a href="/wiki/Kerch_Strait" title="Kerch Strait">Kerch Straits</a> to retake <a href="/wiki/Kerch" title="Kerch">Kerch</a> and its peninsula, and on 30 December executed another landing near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feodosiya" title="Feodosiya">Feodosiya</a>. Only a hurried withdrawal from the area, in contravention of Manstein's orders, by the <a href="/wiki/46th_Infantry_Division_(Wehrmacht)" title="46th Infantry Division (Wehrmacht)">46th Infantry Division</a> under General <a href="/wiki/Hans_Graf_von_Sponeck" title="Hans Graf von Sponeck">Hans Graf von Sponeck</a> prevented a collapse of the eastern part of the Crimea; the division lost most of its heavy equipment. Manstein cancelled a planned resumption of the attack and sent most of his forces east to destroy the Soviet bridgehead. The Soviet forces were in a superior position regarding men and materiel as they were able to re-supply by sea, and were therefore pushed by Stalin to conduct further offensives. However, the Soviet troops were unable to capture the critical rail and road access points which would have cut the German lines of supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201021.E2.80.9322_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201021.E2.80.9322-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010238.E2.80.93239.2C_247.2C_252_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010238.E2.80.93239.2C_247.2C_252-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="800" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png/220px-German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png/330px-German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png/440px-German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_Conquest_of_the_Crimea.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map shows progress as of 5 May 1942</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Kerch_Peninsula" title="Battle of the Kerch Peninsula">Battle of the Kerch Peninsula</a>, launched on 8 May 1942, Hitler finally assigned Manstein major air support. The 11th Army was outnumbered on the ground, so Manstein had them feint an attack in the north while the bulk of the force attacked to the south. The Soviet troops were soon fleeing. Manstein recorded in his memoirs the capture of "170,000 prisoners, 1,133 guns and 258 tanks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010259_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010259-67">[67]</a></sup> Kerch was captured on 16 May. The Wehrmacht lost only 8,000 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201023_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201023-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010256.E2.80.93259_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010256.E2.80.93259-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a month's delay, Manstein turned his attention once more to the capture of Sevastopol, a battle in which Germany used some of the largest guns ever built. Along with large numbers of regular artillery pieces, super-heavy 600 mm (24 in) <a href="/wiki/Karl-Ger%C3%A4t" title="Karl-Gerät">Karl-Gerät</a> mortars and the 800 mm (31 in) <a href="/wiki/Schwerer_Gustav" title="Schwerer Gustav">"Dora"</a> <a href="/wiki/Railway_gun" title="Railway gun">railway gun</a> were brought in for the assault. A furious barrage began on the morning of 2 June 1942. All of the resources of the <i><a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <i>Luftflotte 4</i>, commanded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolfram_von_Richthofen" title="Wolfram von Richthofen">Wolfram von Richthofen</a>, were committed; the barrage continued for five days before the ground assault began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201023.E2.80.9324_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201023.E2.80.9324-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantz199594.2C_117_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantz199594.2C_117-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 11th Army gained ground during mid-June, focusing their attention on the northern approaches to the city. Casualties were high on both sides as the month dragged on. Aware of the need to act before the German <a href="/wiki/Case_Blue" title="Case Blue">summer offensive of 1942</a> reduced the availability of reinforcements and supplies, Manstein ordered a surprise attack using amphibious landings across Severnaya Bay on 29 June. The operation was a success; Soviet resistance crumbled. On 1 July German forces entered the city while the Soviet forces conducted a disorganised evacuation, and Hitler promoted Manstein to <i>Generalfeldmarschall</i> the same day. The entire city was in German hands by 4 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantz199594.2C_117_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantz199594.2C_117-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201024.E2.80.9325_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201024.E2.80.9325-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010265.E2.80.93270_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010265.E2.80.93270-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk200891_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk200891-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Crimean campaign, Manstein was indirectly involved in atrocities against the Soviet population, especially those committed by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einsatzgruppe" title="Einsatzgruppe">Einsatzgruppe</a></i> D, one of several <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">Schutzstaffel</a> (SS) groups that had been tasked with the elimination of the Jews of Europe. <i>Einsatzgruppe</i> D travelled in the wake of Manstein's 11th Army, and were provided by Manstein's command with vehicles, fuel, and drivers. Military police cordoned off areas where the <i>Einsatzgruppe</i> planned to shoot Jews to prevent anyone from escaping. Captain Ulrich Gunzert, shocked to have witnessed <i>Einsatzgruppe</i> D massacre a group of Jewish women and children, went to Manstein to ask him to do something to stop the killings. Gunzert states that Manstein told him to forget what he had seen and to concentrate on fighting the Red Army. Gunzert later called Manstein's inaction "a flight from responsibility, a moral failure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200898_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200898-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010288.E2.80.93292_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010288.E2.80.93292-76">[76]</a></sup> Eleven of the seventeen charges against Manstein at his later war crimes trial were related to Nazi maltreatment and killing of Jews and prisoners of war in the Crimea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010240_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010240-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leningrad">Leningrad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Leningrad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Leningrad" title="Siege of Leningrad">Siege of Leningrad</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinyavin_Offensive_(1942)" title="Sinyavin Offensive (1942)">Sinyavin Offensive (1942)</a></div>
<p>After the capture of Sevastopol, Hitler felt Manstein was the right man to command the forces at <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Leningrad" title="Siege of Leningrad">Leningrad</a>, which had been under siege since September 1941. With elements of the 11th Army, Manstein was transferred to the Leningrad front, arriving on 27 August 1942. Manstein again lacked the proper forces to storm the city, so he planned <a href="/wiki/Operation_Nordlicht_(1942)" title="Operation Nordlicht (1942)">Operation Nordlicht</a>, a bold plan for a thrust to cut off Leningrad's supply line at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ladoga" title="Lake Ladoga">Lake Ladoga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201025.E2.80.9326_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201025.E2.80.9326-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, on the day of his arrival, the Red Army launched the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinyavin_Offensive_(1942)" title="Sinyavin Offensive (1942)">Sinyavin Offensive</a>. Originally planned as spoiling attack against <a href="/wiki/Georg_Lindemann" title="Georg Lindemann">Georg Lindemann</a>'s <a href="/wiki/18th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="18th Army (Wehrmacht)">18th Army</a> in the narrow German <a href="/wiki/Salients,_re-entrants_and_pockets" title="Salients, re-entrants and pockets">salient</a> west of Lake Ladoga, the offensive appeared able to break through the German lines, lifting the siege. Hitler, bypassing the usual chain of command, telephoned Manstein directly and ordered him to take offensive action in the area. After a series of heavy battles, he launched a counterattack on 21 September that cut off the two Soviet armies in the salient. Fighting continued throughout October. Although the Soviet offensive was fended off, the resulting attrition meant that the Wehrmacht could no longer execute a decisive assault on Leningrad, and <i>Nordlicht</i> was put on hold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201025.E2.80.9328_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201025.E2.80.9328-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010275.E2.80.93278_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010275.E2.80.93278-80">[80]</a></sup> The siege was finally lifted by the Soviet forces in January 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008621_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008621-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stalingrad">Stalingrad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Stalingrad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stalingrad" title="Battle of Stalingrad">Battle of Stalingrad</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1806" data-file-width="1699" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg/170px-Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg/255px-Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg/340px-Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Battle_of_Stalingrad-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Soviet counterattack at Stalingrad
<div class="legend-line"><span style="display: inline-block; vertical-align: middle; width: 2em; height: 0px; border-style: none; border-top: 2px dotted #000; border-top: solid#dd0000 2px;"> </span> German front, 19 November</div>
<div class="legend-line"><span style="display: inline-block; vertical-align: middle; width: 2em; height: 0px; border-style: none; border-top: 2px dotted #000; border-top: solid#ff6600 2px;"> </span> German front, 12 December</div>
<div class="legend-line"><span style="display: inline-block; vertical-align: middle; width: 2em; height: 0px; border-style: none; border-top: 2px dotted #000; border-top: solid #009a00 2px;"> </span> German front, 24 December</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:solid grey 1px; background-color: #c3c3c3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Russian advance, 19–28 November</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In an attempt to resolve their persistent shortage of oil, the Wehrmacht had launched <i>Fall Blau</i> (<a href="/wiki/Case_Blue" title="Case Blue">Case Blue</a>), a massive offensive aimed against the Caucasian oilfields, in the summer of 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995108.E2.80.93110_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995108.E2.80.93110-82">[82]</a></sup> After German air attacks, the <a href="/wiki/6th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="6th Army (Wehrmacht)">6th Army</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Paulus" title="Friedrich Paulus">Friedrich Paulus</a>, was tasked with capturing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalingrad" title="Stalingrad">Stalingrad</a>, a key city on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Volga" title="River Volga">River Volga</a>. His troops, supported by <a href="/wiki/4th_Panzer_Army" title="4th Panzer Army">4th Panzer Army</a>, entered the city on 12 September. Hand-to-hand combat and street fighting ensued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008409.E2.80.93411_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008409.E2.80.93411-83">[83]</a></sup> The Red Army launched a huge counteroffensive on 19 November, codenamed <a href="/wiki/Operation_Uranus" title="Operation Uranus">Operation Uranus</a>, which was designed to encircle the German armies and trap them in the city; this goal was accomplished on 23 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010282.2C_285_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010282.2C_285-84">[84]</a></sup> Hitler, aware that if Stalingrad were lost it would likely never be retaken, appointed Manstein as commander of the newly created <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_Don" title="Army Group Don">Army Group Don</a> (<i>Heeresgruppe Don</i>), tasked with mounting a relief operation named <i>Unternehmen Wintergewitter</i> (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Winter_Storm" title="Operation Winter Storm">Operation Winter Storm</a>), to reinforce the German hold on the city. Manstein's initial assessment on 24 November was that the 6th Army, given adequate air support, would be able to hold on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008413_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008413-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010287.2C_294_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010287.2C_294-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Launched on 12 December, Winter Storm achieved some initial success. Manstein's three Panzer divisions (comprising the <a href="/wiki/23rd_Panzer_Division_(Wehrmacht)" title="23rd Panzer Division (Wehrmacht)">23rd</a>, <a href="/wiki/6th_Panzer_Division_(Wehrmacht)" title="6th Panzer Division (Wehrmacht)">6th</a>, and <a href="/wiki/17th_Panzer_Division_(Wehrmacht)" title="17th Panzer Division (Wehrmacht)">17th Panzer Divisions</a>) and supporting units of the LVII Panzer Corps advanced to within 48 km (30 mi) of Stalingrad by 20 December at the Myshkova River, where they came under assault by Soviet tanks in blizzard conditions. Manstein made a request to Hitler on 18 December that 6th Army should attempt to break out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010304.E2.80.93305_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010304.E2.80.93305-87">[87]</a></sup> Hitler was against it, and both Manstein and Paulus were reluctant to openly disobey his orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000289_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000289-88">[88]</a></sup> Conditions deteriorated inside the city; the men suffered from lice, the cold weather, and inadequate supplies of food and ammunition. Reichsminister of Aviation <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a> had assured Hitler that the trapped 6th Army could be adequately supplied by air, but due to poor weather, a lack of aircraft, and mechanical difficulties, this turned out not to be the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008413.2C_416.E2.80.93417_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008413.2C_416.E2.80.93417-89">[89]</a></sup> On 24 January, Manstein urged Hitler to allow Paulus to surrender, but he refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010313_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010313-90">[90]</a></sup> In spite of Hitler's wishes, Paulus surrendered with his remaining 91,000 soldiers on 31 January 1943. Some 200,000 German and Romanian soldiers died; of those who surrendered, only 6,000 survivors returned to Germany at the end of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008419.E2.80.93420_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008419.E2.80.93420-91">[91]</a></sup> Manstein believed he had done his best for the 6th Army. The encircled men saw it differently:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>His weakness was that he didn't take a stronger stance against Hitler. One can resign. Or accept the death sentence. If you are totally convinced, and he was, that it was wrong to keep the army in Stalingrad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnopp1998_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnopp1998-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Winrich_Behr" title="Winrich Behr">Winrich Behr</a></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>American historians <a href="/wiki/Williamson_Murray" title="Williamson Murray">Williamson Murray</a> and Allan Millett wrote that Manstein's message to Hitler on 24 November advising him that the 6th Army should not break out, along with Göring's statements that the Luftwaffe could supply Stalingrad, "... sealed the fate of Sixth Army".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000288_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000288-93">[93]</a></sup> Historians, including <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Weinberg" title="Gerhard Weinberg">Gerhard Weinberg</a>, have pointed out that Manstein's version of the events at Stalingrad in his memoir is distorted and several events described there were probably made up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg2005451_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg2005451-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201029.2C_62_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201029.2C_62-95">[95]</a></sup> "Because of the sensitivity of the Stalingrad question in post-war Germany, Manstein worked as hard to distort the record on this matter as on his massive involvement in the murder of Jews", wrote Weinberg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg20051045_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg20051045-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, the Red Army launched an offensive of their own. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Saturn" title="Operation Saturn">Operation Saturn</a> was intended to capture <a href="/wiki/Rostov-on-Don" title="Rostov-on-Don">Rostov</a> and thus cut off the German <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_A" title="Army Group A">Army Group A</a>. However, after the launch of Winter Storm, the Soviet army had to reallocate forces to prevent the relief of Stalingrad, so the operation was scaled down and redubbed "Little Saturn". The offensive forced Manstein to divert forces to avoid the collapse of the entire front. The attack also prevented the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/XLVIII_Panzer_Corps_(Germany)" title="XLVIII Panzer Corps (Germany)">XLVIII Panzer Corps</a> (comprising the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=336th_Infantry_Division_(Wehrmacht)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="336th Infantry Division (Wehrmacht) (page does not exist)">336th Infantry Division</a>, the <a href="/wiki/3rd_Luftwaffe_Field_Division_(Germany)" title="3rd Luftwaffe Field Division (Germany)">3rd Luftwaffe Field Division</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/11th_Panzer_Division_(Wehrmacht)" title="11th Panzer Division (Wehrmacht)">11th Panzer Division</a>), under the command of General <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Knobelsdorff" title="Otto von Knobelsdorff">Otto von Knobelsdorff</a>, from joining up with the LVII Panzer Corps as planned to aid the relief effort. Instead, the XLVIII Panzer Corps held a line along the <a href="/wiki/Chir_River" title="Chir River">Chir River</a>, beating off successive Soviet attacks. General <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Balck" title="Hermann Balck">Hermann Balck</a> used the 11th Panzer Division to counterattack Soviet salients. On the verge of collapse, the German units were able to hold the line, but the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Army_in_Russia" title="Italian Army in Russia">Italian 8th Army</a> on the flanks was overwhelmed and subsequently destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENipe200018.E2.80.9333_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENipe200018.E2.80.9333-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995143.E2.80.93147_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995143.E2.80.93147-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spurred on by this success, the Red Army planned a series of follow-up offensives in January and February 1943 intended to decisively beat the German forces in southern Russia. After the destruction of the remaining Hungarian and Italian forces during the <a href="/wiki/Ostrogozhsk%E2%80%93Rossosh_Offensive" title="Ostrogozhsk–Rossosh Offensive">Ostrogozhsk–Rossosh Offensive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Star" title="Operation Star">Operation Star</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Gallop" title="Operation Gallop">Operation Gallop</a> were launched to recapture <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kharkov" title="Kharkov">Kharkov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kursk" title="Kursk">Kursk</a> and to cut off all German forces east of <a href="/wiki/Donetsk" title="Donetsk">Donetsk</a>. Those operations succeeded in breaking through the German lines and threatened the whole southern part of the German front. To deal with this threat, Army Group Don, <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_B" title="Army Group B">Army Group B</a>, and parts of Army Group A were united as <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_South" title="Army Group South">Army Group South</a> (<i>Heeresgruppe Süd</i>) under Manstein's command in early February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995143.E2.80.93147_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995143.E2.80.93147-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENipe200054.E2.80.9364.2C_110_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENipe200054.E2.80.9364.2C_110-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kharkov_operation">Kharkov operation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kharkov operation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Kharkov" title="Third Battle of Kharkov">Third Battle of Kharkov</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A,_Ostfront,_Adolf_Hitler,_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="515" data-file-width="800" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A%2C_Ostfront%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A%2C_Ostfront%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A%2C_Ostfront%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A%2C_Ostfront%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A%2C_Ostfront%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A%2C_Ostfront%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1995-041-23A,_Ostfront,_Adolf_Hitler,_Erich_v._Manstein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
On 10 March 1943, under heavy security, Hitler flew to Army Group South's headquarters at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zaporozh%27ye" title="Zaporozh'ye">Zaporozh'ye</a>, Ukraine, only 48 km (30 mi) from the front lines, to review the military situation. Manstein greets Hitler upon his arrival at the local airfield; on the right are <a href="/wiki/Hans_Baur" title="Hans Baur">Hans Baur</a> and the Luftwaffe Generalfeldmarschall <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolfram_von_Richthofen" title="Wolfram von Richthofen">Wolfram von Richthofen</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
