<p>During their offensives in February 1943, the Red Army broke through the German lines, retaking Kursk on 9 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010333_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010333-100">[100]</a></sup> As Army Groups B and Don were in danger of being surrounded, Manstein repeatedly called for reinforcements. Although Hitler called on 13 February for Kharkov to be held "at all costs",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010333_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010333-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/SS-Oberst-Gruppenf%C3%BChrer" title="SS-Oberst-Gruppenführer">SS-Oberst-Gruppenführer</a> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hausser" title="Paul Hausser">Paul Hausser</a>, commander of the <a href="/wiki/II_SS_Panzer_Corps" title="II SS Panzer Corps">II SS Panzer Corps</a>, ordered the city evacuated on 15 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010334_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010334-101">[101]</a></sup> Hitler arrived at the front in person on 17 February, and over the course of three days of exhausting meetings, Manstein convinced him that offensive action was needed in the area to regain the initiative and prevent encirclement. Troops were reorganised and reinforcements were pulled into the zone from neighbouring armies. Manstein immediately began planning a counteroffensive, launched on 20 February, that later became known as the "backhand blow"; Vatutin and the Soviet forces, believing that Manstein would retreat, were taken completely by surprise. By 2 March, the Wehrmacht had captured 615 tanks and had killed some 23,000 Soviet soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010338.E2.80.93341.2C_344_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010338.E2.80.93341.2C_344-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>To reinforce the point that the recapture of Kharkov was important politically, Hitler returned to the front on 10 March. Manstein carefully assembled his available forces along a wide front to prevent their encirclement and recaptured Kharkov on 14 March, after bloody street fighting in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Kharkov" title="Third Battle of Kharkov">Third Battle of Kharkov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010343_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010343-103">[103]</a></sup> For this accomplishment, he received the Oak Leaves for the Knight's Cross.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManstein2004565_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManstein2004565-104">[104]</a></sup> Hausser's II SS Panzer Corps captured <a href="/wiki/Belgorod" title="Belgorod">Belgorod</a> on 18 March. Manstein's counteroffensive had not only prevented the disintegration of the entire front, it had regained substantial territory and resulted in the destruction of three Soviet armies and the retreat of three others. Soviet casualties for the preceding month in that sector had been 46,000 dead and 14,000 taken prisoner. Captured or destroyed were 600 tanks and 1,200 pieces of artillery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010346_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010346-105">[105]</a></sup> The spring thaw began by 23 March, ending operations in the area for the time being. Planning was then undertaken to eliminate the enemy salient at Kursk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010343.E2.80.93344_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010343.E2.80.93344-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operation_Citadel">Operation Citadel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Operation Citadel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kursk" title="Battle of Kursk">Battle of Kursk</a></div>
<p>Manstein favoured an immediate pincer attack on the Kursk salient after the battle at Kharkov, but Hitler was concerned that such a plan would draw forces away from the industrial region in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donets_Basin" title="Donets Basin">Donets Basin</a>. In any event, the ground was still <a href="/wiki/Rasputitsa" title="Rasputitsa">too muddy</a> to move the tanks into position. In lieu of an immediate attack, the OKH prepared Operation Citadel, the launching of which would be delayed while more troops were gathered in the area and the mud solidified. Meanwhile, the Red Army, well aware of the danger of encirclement, also moved in large numbers of reinforcements, and their intelligence reports revealed the expected locations and timing of the German thrusts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010350.E2.80.93351_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010350.E2.80.93351-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008485_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008485-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kursk_south.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Kursk_south.svg/220px-Kursk_south.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Kursk_south.svg/330px-Kursk_south.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Kursk_south.svg/440px-Kursk_south.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kursk_south.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Battle of Kursk: southern pincer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Citadel was the last German strategic offensive on the Eastern Front, and one of the largest battles in history, involving more than four million men. By the time the Wehrmacht launched their initial assault on 5 July 1943, the Soviet forces outnumbered them by nearly three to one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008486_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008486-109">[109]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walther_Model" title="Walther Model">Walther Model</a> was in command of the northern pincer, with the <a href="/wiki/9th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="9th Army (Wehrmacht)">Ninth Army</a>, while Manstein's Army Group South formed the southern pincer. Both armies were slowed as the tanks were blown up in minefields and caught up in combat on a series of prepared Soviet defensive lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008487_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008487-110">[110]</a></sup> After five days of fighting Model's advance was stopped, with the Ninth Panzers suffering 25,000 casualties. By 13 July Model's forces were being drawn away towards Orel, where the Soviet army had launched <a href="/wiki/Operation_Kutuzov" title="Operation Kutuzov">Operation Kutuzov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantzHouse1999217_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantzHouse1999217-111">[111]</a></sup> Manstein's forces were able to penetrate the Soviet lines, causing heavy casualties. He reached, but did not enter or capture, <a href="/wiki/Prokhorovka,_Belgorod_Oblast" title="Prokhorovka, Belgorod Oblast">Prokhorovka</a>, his first major objective, on 11 July, inflicting serious Soviet losses in the resulting <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Prokhorovka" title="Battle of Prokhorovka">Battle of Prokhorovka</a>. However, on 13 July Hitler called off the failed Kursk offensive; the Allies had <a href="/wiki/Allied_invasion_of_Sicily" title="Allied invasion of Sicily">landed in Sicily</a>, so he issued the order for a withdrawal. Manstein protested; he felt that the Soviet forces had exhausted all their reserves in the area, and he did not want to stop until all his own reserves had been committed. Hitler, however, insisted on calling off the operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantzHouse1999218_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantzHouse1999218-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010377.E2.80.93378_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010377.E2.80.93378-113">[113]</a></sup> Although Soviet casualties were indeed heavy, modern historians discount the possibility of a successful German continuation of the offensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201041.E2.80.9345_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201041.E2.80.9345-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000298_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000298-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995160.E2.80.93167_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantz1995160.E2.80.93167-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Kursk_to_the_Dnieper">From Kursk to the Dnieper</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: From Kursk to the Dnieper">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Belgorod-Khar%27kov_Offensive_Operation" title="Belgorod-Khar'kov Offensive Operation">Belgorod-Khar'kov Offensive Operation</a></div>
<p>Manstein regarded the Battle of Kursk as something of a German victory, as he believed that he had destroyed much of the Red Army's offensive capacity for the rest of 1943. This assessment turned out to be incorrect, as the Red Army was able to recover much more quickly than Manstein expected. Manstein moved his panzer reserves to the <a href="/wiki/Mius_River" title="Mius River">Mius River</a> and the lower Dnieper, not realising the Soviet activities there were a diversion. A Soviet offensive that began on 3 August put Army Group South under heavy pressure. After two days of heavy fighting, the Soviet troops broke through the German lines and retook <a href="/wiki/Belgorod" title="Belgorod">Belgorod</a>, punching a 56 km (35 mi) wide hole between Fourth Panzer Army and <a href="/wiki/German_Army_Detachment_Kempf" title="German Army Detachment Kempf">Armee Abteilung <i>Kempf</i></a>, tasked with holding Kharkov. In response to Manstein's demands for reinforcements, Hitler sent the <a href="/wiki/Panzer-Grenadier-Division_Gro%C3%9Fdeutschland" title="Panzer-Grenadier-Division Großdeutschland"><i>Großdeutschland</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/7th_Panzer_Division_(Wehrmacht)" title="7th Panzer Division (Wehrmacht)">7th Panzers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2nd_SS_Division_Das_Reich" title="2nd SS Division Das Reich">SS 2nd <i>Das Reich</i></a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3rd_SS_Division_Totenkopf" title="3rd SS Division Totenkopf">SS 3rd <i>Totenkopf</i></a> Divisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000390.E2.80.93391_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000390.E2.80.93391-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201041.E2.80.9347_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201041.E2.80.9347-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010384.E2.80.93385_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010384.E2.80.93385-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06,_Ukraine,_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="745" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06%2C_Ukraine%2C_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06%2C_Ukraine%2C_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06%2C_Ukraine%2C_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06%2C_Ukraine%2C_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06%2C_Ukraine%2C_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06%2C_Ukraine%2C_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-705-0262-06,_Ukraine,_von_Manstein_und_Speidel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Manstein (right) with <i>Generalmajor</i> <a href="/wiki/Hans_Speidel" title="Hans Speidel">Hans Speidel</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Dnieper" title="Dnieper">Dnieper</a>, September 1943</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Construction began of defensive positions along the Dnieper, but Hitler refused requests to pull back, insisting that Kharkov be held. With reinforcements trickling in, Manstein waged a series of counterattacks and armoured battles near <a href="/wiki/Bohodukhiv" title="Bohodukhiv">Bohodukhiv</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okhtyrka" title="Okhtyrka">Okhtyrka</a> between 13 and 17 August, which resulted in heavy casualties as they ran into prepared Soviet lines. On 20 August he informed the OKH that his forces in the Donets river area were holding a too-wide front with insufficient numbers, and that he needed to either withdraw to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dnieper_River" title="Dnieper River">Dnieper River</a> or receive reinforcements. Continuous pressure from the Soviet forces had separated Army Group Centre from Army Group South and severely threatened Manstein's northern flank. When the Red Army threw their main reserves behind a drive to retake Kharkov on 21–22 August, Manstein took advantage of this to close the gap between the 4th Panzer and 8th Armies and reestablish a defensive line. Hitler finally allowed Manstein to withdraw back across the Dnieper on 15 September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201041.E2.80.9347_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201041.E2.80.9347-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000391.E2.80.93392_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000391.E2.80.93392-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010386.E2.80.93394_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010386.E2.80.93394-121">[121]</a></sup> During the withdrawal, Manstein ordered <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a> actions to be taken in a zone 20 to 30 kilometres (12 to 19 mi) from the river, and later faced charges at his war crimes trial for issuing this order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010396.2C_471_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010396.2C_471-122">[122]</a></sup> Soviet losses in July and August included over 1.6 million casualties, 10,000 tanks and self-propelled artillery pieces, and 4,200 aircraft. German losses, while only one-tenth that of the Soviet losses, were much more difficult to sustain, as there were no further reserves of men and materiel to draw on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2008489.E2.80.93490_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2008489.E2.80.93490-123">[123]</a></sup> In a series of four meetings that September, Manstein tried unsuccessfully to convince Hitler to reorganise the high command and let his generals make more of the military decisions. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010387.E2.80.93392_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010387.E2.80.93392-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dnieper_campaign">Dnieper campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Dnieper campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Dnieper" title="Battle of the Dnieper">Battle of the Dnieper</a></div>
<p>In September 1943 Manstein withdrew to the west bank of the Dnieper in an operation that for the most part was well-ordered, but at times degenerated into a disorganised rout as his exhausted soldiers became "unglued".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000393_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000393-125">[125]</a></sup> Hundreds of thousands of Soviet civilians travelled west with them, many bringing livestock and personal property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010397_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010397-126">[126]</a></sup> Manstein correctly deduced that the next Soviet attack would be towards <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a>, but as had been the case throughout the campaign, the Red Army used <i><a href="/wiki/Russian_military_deception" title="Russian military deception">maskirovka</a></i> (deception) to disguise the timing and exact location of their intended offensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010399_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010399-127">[127]</a></sup> Historians <a href="/wiki/Williamson_Murray" title="Williamson Murray">Williamson Murray</a> and Allan Reed Millett wrote that many German generals' "fanatical belief" in <a href="/wiki/Nazism_and_race" title="Nazism and race">Nazi racial theories</a> " ... made the idea that Slavs could manipulate German intelligence with such consistency utterly inconceivable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000395_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000395-128">[128]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1st_Ukrainian_Front" title="1st Ukrainian Front">1st Ukrainian Front</a>, led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolai_Fyodorovich_Vatutin" title="Nikolai Fyodorovich Vatutin">Nikolai Fyodorovich Vatutin</a>, met the outnumbered Fourth Panzer Army near Kiev. Vatutin first made a thrust near Liutezh, just north of Kiev, and then attacked near Bukrin, to the south, on 1 November. The German troops, thinking Bukrin would be the location of the main attack, were taken completely by surprise when Vatutin captured the bridgehead at Liutezh and gained a foothold on the west bank of the Dnieper. Kiev was liberated on 6 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010402_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010402-129">[129]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/17th_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="17th Army (Wehrmacht)">17th Army</a> was cut off and isolated in the Crimea by the attacking <a href="/wiki/4th_Ukrainian_Front" title="4th Ukrainian Front">4th Ukrainian Front</a> on 28 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010400.2C_Map_15_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010400.2C_Map_15-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="956" data-file-width="1256" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg/220px-Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg/330px-Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg/440px-Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_dnieper_battle_grand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Operations along the Dnieper, July to December 1943</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the guidance of General <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Balck" title="Hermann Balck">Hermann Balck</a>, the cities of <a href="/wiki/Zhytomyr" title="Zhytomyr">Zhytomyr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korosten" title="Korosten">Korosten</a> were retaken in mid-November,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010402_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010402-129">[129]</a></sup> but after receiving reinforcements Vatutin resumed the offensive on 24 December 1943,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarratt201221.E2.80.9322_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarratt201221.E2.80.9322-131">[131]</a></sup> and the Red Army continued its successful advance. Manstein's repeated requests to Hitler for more reinforcements were turned down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010402.2C_404.2C_411_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010402.2C_404.2C_411-132">[132]</a></sup> On 4 January 1944 Manstein met with Hitler to tell him that the Dnieper line was untenable and that he needed to retreat in order to save his forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000396_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000396-133">[133]</a></sup> Hitler refused, and Manstein again requested changes in the highest levels of the military leadership, but was turned down, as Hitler believed that he alone was capable of managing the wider strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010410_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010410-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January Manstein was forced to retreat further west by the Soviet offensive. Without waiting for permission from Hitler, he ordered the German <a href="/wiki/XI_Army_Corps_(Wehrmacht)" title="XI Army Corps (Wehrmacht)">XI</a> and XXXXII Corps (consisting of 56,000 men in six divisions) of Army Group South to break out of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korsun_Pocket" title="Korsun Pocket">Korsun Pocket</a> during the night of 16–17 February 1944. By the beginning of March, the Soviet forces had driven the Wehrmacht well back of the river. Because of Hitler's directive of 19 March that from that point forward all positions were to be defended to the last man, Manstein's <a href="/wiki/1st_Panzer_Army" title="1st Panzer Army">1st Panzer Army</a> became encircled on 21 March when permission to break out was not received from Hitler in time. Manstein flew to Hitler's headquarters in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lvov" title="Lvov">Lvov</a> to try to convince him to change his mind. Hitler eventually relented, but relieved Manstein of his command on 30 March 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010414.E2.80.93418_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010414.E2.80.93418-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein appeared on the cover of the 10 January 1944 issue of <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i> magazine</a>, above the caption "Retreat may be masterly, but victory is in the opposite direction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010412_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010412-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.271944_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.271944-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dismissal">Dismissal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dismissal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Manstein received the Swords of the Knight's Cross on 30 March 1944<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScherzer2007503_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScherzer2007503-138">[138]</a></sup> and handed over control of Army Group South to Model on 2 April during a meeting at Hitler's mountain retreat, the <a href="/wiki/Berghof_(residence)" title="Berghof (residence)">Berghof</a>. Model's adjutant, Günther Reichhelm, later described the scene and Manstein's response:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>He must have paid him compliments about his strategic skills during the attack operations, but he also said, "I cannot use you in the South. Field Marshal Model will take over." And Manstein replied, "My Führer ... please believe me when I say I will use all strategic means at my disposal to defend the soil in which my son lies buried."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnopp1998_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnopp1998-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Günther Reichhelm, Model's adjutant</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>While on medical leave after surgery to remove a cataract in his right eye, Manstein recovered at home in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liegnitz" title="Liegnitz">Liegnitz</a> and in a medical facility in <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>. He suffered from an infection and for a time was in danger of losing his sight. On the day of the failed <a href="/wiki/20_July_plot" title="20 July plot">20 July plot</a>, an assassination attempt on Hitler's life that was part of a planned <a href="/wiki/Operation_Valkyrie" title="Operation Valkyrie">military <i>coup d'état</i></a>, Manstein was at a seaside resort on the Baltic. Although he had met at various times with three of the main conspirators— <a href="/wiki/Claus_von_Stauffenberg" title="Claus von Stauffenberg">Claus von Stauffenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henning_von_Tresckow" title="Henning von Tresckow">Henning von Tresckow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Christoph_Freiherr_von_Gersdorff" title="Rudolf Christoph Freiherr von Gersdorff">Rudolf Christoph Freiherr von Gersdorff</a>—Manstein was not involved in the conspiracy; he later said <i>"Preussische Feldmarschälle meutern nicht"</i> – "Prussian field marshals do not mutiny."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeevor1999276_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeevor1999276-139">[139]</a></sup> Still, the <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a> placed Manstein's house under surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010420.E2.80.93425_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010420.E2.80.93425-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>When it became obvious that Hitler would not be appointing him to a new post, Manstein bought an estate in East Pomerania in October 1944, but was soon forced to abandon it as Soviet forces overran the area. His home at Liegnitz had to be evacuated on 22 January 1945, and he and his family took refuge temporarily with friends in Berlin. While there, Manstein tried to get an audience with Hitler in the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrerbunker" title="Führerbunker">Führerbunker</a>, but was turned away. He and his family continued to move further west into Germany until the war in Europe ended with a German defeat in May 1945. Manstein suffered further complications in his right eye and was receiving treatment in a hospital in <a href="/wiki/Heiligenhafen" title="Heiligenhafen">Heiligenhafen</a> when he was arrested by the British and transferred to a prisoner of war camp near <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg" title="Lüneburg">Lüneburg</a> on 26 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201058.E2.80.9360_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201058.E2.80.9360-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000401_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayMillett2000401-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010425.E2.80.93431_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010425.E2.80.93431-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial">Trial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Trial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Erich_von_Manstein" title="Trial of Erich von Manstein">Trial of Erich von Manstein</a></div>
<p>Manstein was moved to <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> in October 1945. He was held at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palace_of_Justice_(Nuremberg)" title="Palace of Justice (Nuremberg)">Palace of Justice</a>, the location of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg Trials</a> of major Nazi war criminals and organisations. While there, Manstein helped prepare a 132-page document for the defence of the General Staff and the OKW, on trial at Nuremberg in August 1946. The myth that the Wehrmacht was "clean"—not culpable for the events of the Holocaust—arose partly as a result of this document, written largely by Manstein, along with General of Cavalry <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Westphal" title="Siegfried Westphal">Siegfried Westphal</a>. He also gave oral testimony about the <i>Einsatzgruppen</i>, the treatment of prisoners of war, and the concept of military obedience, especially as related to the <a href="/wiki/Commissar_Order" title="Commissar Order">Commissar Order</a>, an order issued by Hitler in 1941, requiring all Soviet <a href="/wiki/Political_commissar" title="Political commissar">political commissars</a> to be shot without trial. Manstein admitted that he received the order, but said he did not carry it out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010432.E2.80.93434_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010432.E2.80.93434-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Documents from 1941 presented at Nuremberg and at Manstein's own later trial contradict this claim: He actually received regular reports throughout that summer regarding the execution of hundreds of political commissars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010252_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010252-144">[144]</a></sup> He denied any knowledge of the activities of the <i>Einsatzgruppen</i>, and testified that soldiers under his command were not involved in the murder of Jewish civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010440.E2.80.93448_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010440.E2.80.93448-145">[145]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Otto_Ohlendorf" title="Otto Ohlendorf">Otto Ohlendorf</a>, commander of <i>Einsatzgruppe</i> D, contradicted this during his testimony, saying that not only was Manstein aware of what was happening but that the Eleventh Army was involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010270.E2.80.93271_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010270.E2.80.93271-146">[146]</a></sup> In September 1946, the General Staff and the OKW were declared to not be criminal organisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010448_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010448-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>After his testimony at Nuremberg, Manstein was interned by the British as a prisoner of war at <a href="/wiki/Island_Farm" title="Island Farm">Island Farm</a> (also known as Special Camp 11) in <a href="/wiki/Bridgend" title="Bridgend">Bridgend</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, where he awaited the decision as to whether or not he would face a war crimes trial. He mostly kept apart from the other inmates, taking solitary walks, tending a small garden, and beginning work on the drafts of two books. British author <a href="/wiki/B._H._Liddell_Hart" title="B. H. Liddell Hart">B. H. Liddell Hart</a> was in correspondence with Manstein and others at Island Farm and visited inmates of several camps around Britain while preparing his best-selling 1947 book <i>On the Other Side of the Hill</i>. Liddell Hart was an admirer of the German generals; he described Manstein as an operational genius. The two remained in contact, and Liddell Hart later helped Manstein arrange the publication of the English edition of his memoir, <i><a href="/wiki/Verlorene_Siege" title="Verlorene Siege">Verlorene Siege</a></i> (<i>Lost Victories</i>), in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies2008102_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies2008102-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010452.E2.80.93456_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010452.E2.80.93456-149">[149]</a></sup> The British cabinet, under pressure from the Soviet Union, finally decided in July 1948 to prosecute Manstein for war crimes. He and three other senior officers (<a href="/wiki/Walther_von_Brauchitsch" title="Walther von Brauchitsch">Walther von Brauchitsch</a>, Gerd von Rundstedt and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Strauss" title="Adolf Strauss">Adolf Strauss</a>) were transferred to <i><a href="/wiki/Munster_Training_Area" title="Munster Training Area">Munsterlager</a></i> to await trial. Brauchitsch died that October and Rundstedt and Strauss were released on medical grounds in March 1949. Manstein's trial was held in Hamburg from 23 August to 19 December 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010460.E2.80.93463.2C_467_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010460.E2.80.93463.2C_467-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein faced seventeen charges at the trial, three of which pertained to events in Poland and fourteen regarding events in the Soviet Union. Charges included maltreatment of prisoners of war, cooperation with the <i>Einsatzgruppe</i> D in killing Jewish residents of the Crimea and disregarding the welfare of civilians by using "scorched earth" tactics, while retreating from the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010469.E2.80.93473_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010469.E2.80.93473-151">[151]</a></sup> The prosecution, led by senior counsel <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Comyns_Carr" title="Arthur Comyns Carr">Arthur Comyns Carr</a>, used an order Manstein had signed on 20 November 1941, based on the <a href="/wiki/Severity_Order" title="Severity Order">Severity Order</a> that had been issued by Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Walther_von_Reichenau" title="Walther von Reichenau">Walther von Reichenau</a>, to build their case, that Manstein had known about and was complicit with the genocide. The order called for the elimination of the "Jewish Bolshevik system" and the "harsh punishment of Jewry". Manstein claimed that he remembered asking for a draft of such an order but had no recollection of signing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010243.2C_466.2C_475_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010243.2C_466.2C_475-152">[152]</a></sup> American historians <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Smelser" title="Ronald Smelser">Ronald Smelser</a> and Edward Davies wrote in 2008, that Manstein agreed with Hitler's idea that the war against the Soviet Union was a war to exterminate Judeo-Bolshevism and that he committed perjury, when he claimed not to remember signing his version of the Severity Order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200898_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200898-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein's defence, led by the prominent lawyer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reginald_Thomas_Paget" title="Reginald Thomas Paget">Reginald Thomas Paget</a>, argued that the order was justified because many partisans were Jews and so Manstein's order calling for all Jews to be executed was justified, by his desire to protect his men from partisan attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200898_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200898-75">[75]</a></sup> He argued that Manstein was not compelled to disobey orders given by his sovereign government, even if such orders were illegal. Manstein, speaking in his defence, stated that he found the Nazi racial policy to be repugnant. Sixteen other witnesses testified that Manstein had no knowledge of or involvement in genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010466.2C_477.E2.80.93480_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010466.2C_477.E2.80.93480-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaget1952230_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaget1952230-154">[154]</a></sup> Paget called the "Russians" "savages", arguing that Manstein showed restraint as a "decent German soldier", in upholding the laws of war when fighting against the "Russians", who displayed the most "appalling savagery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies2008101_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies2008101-155">[155]</a></sup> Whether or not Manstein was responsible for the activities of <i>Einsatzgruppe</i> D, a unit not under his direct control but operating in his zone of command, became one of the key points of the trial, the prosecution claimed that it was Manstein's duty to know about the activities of this unit and also his duty to put a stop to their genocidal operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010475.E2.80.93477_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010475.E2.80.93477-156">[156]</a></sup> Recent scholars, including Benoît Lemay, are of the opinion that he almost certainly perjured himself at his trial and at Nuremberg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010265_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010265-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200843_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200843-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein was found guilty on nine of the charges and was sentenced to eighteen years in prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010467.E2.80.93468_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010467.E2.80.93468-159">[159]</a></sup> An uproar ensued among Manstein's supporters in Britain and in Germany. Liddell Hart lobbied in the British press, and in Germany the sentence was seen as a political decision. The sentence was reduced to 12 years in February 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010469.E2.80.93470_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010469.E2.80.93470-160">[160]</a></sup> Paget published a best-selling book in 1951 about Manstein's career and trial, which portrayed Manstein as an honourable soldier fighting heroically despite overwhelming odds on the Eastern Front and who had been convicted of crimes that he did not commit. The book helped to contribute to the growing cult surrounding Manstein's name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies2008101.E2.80.93102_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies2008101.E2.80.93102-161">[161]</a></sup> His release on 7 May 1953 was partly a result of a recurrence of his eye problems but also the result of pressure by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Adenauer" title="Konrad Adenauer">Konrad Adenauer</a>, Liddell Hart, Paget and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010492.E2.80.93493_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010492.E2.80.93493-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010470.E2.80.93471_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010470.E2.80.93471-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antisemitism">Antisemitism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Antisemitism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">Antisemitism</a> was common in Germany and throughout Europe during this period, and Manstein's attitude towards the Jews had its roots in his exposure to and assimilation of these views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010262_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010262-164">[164]</a></sup> His actions were a reflection of his loyalty toward Hitler and the Nazi regime and of his grounding in a sense of duty based on traditional Prussian military values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010260_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010260-165">[165]</a></sup> His criticism of Hitler was based solely on their disagreements over the conduct of the war, not about the regime's racial policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk201061.E2.80.9362_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk201061.E2.80.9362-166">[166]</a></sup> Some historians, including <a href="/wiki/Antony_Beevor" title="Antony Beevor">Antony Beevor</a> and Benoît Lemay, are of the opinion that Manstein had some Jewish ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeevor199916_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeevor199916-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201036.E2.80.9337_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201036.E2.80.9337-168">[168]</a></sup> Manstein was the only Reichswehr officer who opposed the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Aryan_paragraph" title="Aryan paragraph">Aryan paragraph</a> in 1934. He sent a letter of protest to General Beck, commenting that anyone who had volunteered to serve in the armed forces had already proven their worth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201034.E2.80.9335_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201034.E2.80.9335-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lemay speculated that Manstein may have been interested in protecting his two <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mischlinge" title="Mischlinge">Mischlinge</a></i> grandnephews who were already serving in the Reichswehr. He may have also been concerned about the possibility that he had distant Jewish ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201036.E2.80.9337_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201036.E2.80.9337-168">[168]</a></sup> The SS carried out an investigation into Manstein's ancestry but the report was not completed and the results remain unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay201036.E2.80.9337_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay201036.E2.80.9337-168">[168]</a></sup> On the other hand, Manstein believed that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevism" title="Bolshevism">Bolshevism</a> and Jews were <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Bolshevism" title="Jewish Bolshevism">inextricably linked</a>, that there was a global conspiracy led by the Jews, and that in order to stop the spread of communism it was necessary to remove the Jews from European society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010259.2C_262.E2.80.93263_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010259.2C_262.E2.80.93263-170">[170]</a></sup> His order of 20 November 1941, based on the Severity Order of Reichenau, reads in part:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Jewish Bolshevik system must be wiped out once and for all and should never again be allowed to invade our European living space ... It is the same Jewish class of beings who have done so much damage to our own Fatherland by virtue of their activities against the nation and civilisation, and who promote anti-German tendencies throughout the world, and who will be the harbingers of revenge. Their extermination is a dictate of our own survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010243_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010243-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurleigh2000522_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurleigh2000522-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Manstein did nothing to prevent the killing of Jews and other civilians in the areas where his units were operating, and in which his Eleventh Army actively participated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010271.2C_278_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010271.2C_278-173">[173]</a></sup> That Manstein was well aware of the <i>Einsatzgruppen</i> massacres is demonstrated by a 1941 letter he sent to <a href="/wiki/Otto_Ohlendorf" title="Otto Ohlendorf">Otto Ohlendorf</a>, in which Manstein demands Ohlendorf hand over the wristwatches of murdered Jews. Manstein felt his men deserved the watches, since they were doing so much to help Ohlendorf's men with their work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200843_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200843-158">[158]</a></sup> Smelser and Davies note that this letter was the only time that Manstein ever complained about the activities of the <i>Einsatzgruppen</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200843_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200843-158">[158]</a></sup> Manstein later stated that he felt the number of Jews killed in the Holocaust was exaggerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKale2012338_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKale2012338-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_life_and_memoirs">Post-war life and memoirs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Post-war life and memoirs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Along with ten other former senior officers, Manstein was called on in 1955 by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Ministry_of_Defence_(Germany)" title="Federal Ministry of Defence (Germany)">Amt Blank</a> to formulate plans for the re-founding of the German army. On 20 June 1953, he spoke to the <i><a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">Bundestag</a></i>, giving his analysis of strategic power considerations and the country's defence and spoke about whether the country should have a professional army or a conscripted army. His opinion was that the length of service for <i><a href="/wiki/Bundeswehr" title="Bundeswehr">Bundeswehr</a></i> conscripts should be at least 18 months, preferably 24 months. His idea to form a reserve force was later implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010499.2C_498_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010499.2C_498-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000170_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnopp2000170-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein's war memoir, <i><a href="/wiki/Verlorene_Siege" title="Verlorene Siege">Verlorene Siege</a></i> (<i>Lost Victories</i>), was published in West Germany in 1955 and translated into English in 1958 for worldwide distribution. The book was a highly acclaimed best-seller, critical of Hitler and his leadership style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200890.2C_95_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200890.2C_95-177">[177]</a></sup> Historians such as Liddell Hart saw Manstein's emphasis on the purely military aspects of the war, while ignoring the political and moral aspects, as a way for him to absolve himself and the high command of any responsibility for the events of the Holocaust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010475.2C_478_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010475.2C_478-178">[178]</a></sup> His favourable portrayal of himself influenced popular opinion; he became the centre of a military cult which cast him not only as one of Germany's greatest generals, but also one of the greatest in history. He has been described as a <i>militärische Kult- und Leitfigur</i> ("military cult legend"), a general of legendary — almost mythical — ability, much honoured by both the public and historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200890.E2.80.9392_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmelserDavies200890.E2.80.9392-179">[179]</a></sup> Biographers, including Benoît Lemay, feel that his narrow focus on military matters to the exclusion of moral issues cannot be considered ethical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010480.E2.80.93482_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010480.E2.80.93482-180">[180]</a></sup> In 1967 Lieutenant General Ernst Ferber of the <i>Bundeswehr</i> encouraged young German soldiers to eschew unconditional obedience to the head of state, and to serve the nation and the German people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemay2010489.E2.80.93490_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemay2010489.E2.80.93490-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Manstein and his wife moved several times after his release from prison, living in <a href="/wiki/Essen" title="Essen">Essen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a> for a time before settling into a house near Munich in 1958. The second volume of his memoirs, <i>Aus einem Soldatenleben</i> ("A Soldier's Life"), covering the period from 1887 to 1939, was published in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010495.2C_498_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010495.2C_498-182">[182]</a></sup> His wife, Jutta Sibylle von Manstein, died in 1966. Erich von Manstein died of a stroke on the night of 9 June 1973 at the age of 85. As the last but one surviving German field marshal (<a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Sch%C3%B6rner" title="Ferdinand Schörner">Ferdinand Schörner</a> died 2 July 1973), he was buried with full military honours, his funeral being attended by hundreds of soldiers of all ranks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010503_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010503-183">[183]</a></sup> His obituary in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> states "His influence and effect came from powers of mind and depth of knowledge rather than by generating an electrifying current among the troops or 'putting over' his personality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27The_Times.27.27.2C_13_June_1973_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27The_Times.27.27.2C_13_June_1973-184">[184]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiegel_(magazine)" title="Spiegel (magazine)">Spiegel</a></i> magazine was much harsher, saying "He assisted in the march to catastrophe—misled by a blind sense of duty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010504_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelvin2010504-185">[185]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEForczyk20107.E2.80.939-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForczyk20107.E2.80.939_21-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFForczyk2010">Forczyk 2010</a>, pp. 7–9.</span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_von_Manstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>Official records</b></p>
<ul>
<li>The British records of the Manstein trial are now housed in the <a href="/wiki/Liddell_Hart_Centre_for_Military_Archives" title="Liddell Hart Centre for Military Archives">Liddell Hart Centre for Military Archives</a>, <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">King's College London</a>.</li>
<li>Manstein's testimony at Nuremberg is contained in three files at the <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> <a href="/wiki/Avalon_Project" title="Avalon Project">Avalon Project</a>: <a class="external text" href="http://avalon.law.yale.edu/imt/08-09-46.asp" rel="nofollow">9 August 1946</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://avalon.law.yale.edu/imt/08-10-46.asp" rel="nofollow">10 August 1946</a>, and <a class="external text" href="http://avalon.law.yale.edu/imt/08-12-46.asp" rel="nofollow">12 August 1946</a>.</li>
</ul>

München: Bernard &amp; Graefe. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3-7637-5214-5" title="Special:BookSources/3-7637-5214-5">3-7637-5214-5</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AErich+von+Manstein&amp;rft.aufirst=Erich&amp;rft.aulast=Manstein&amp;rft.btitle=Soldat+im+20.+Jahrhundert&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=3-7637-5214-5&amp;rft.place=M%C3%BCnchen&amp;rft.pub=Bernard+%26+Graefe&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> (first published in 1958 as <i>Aus einem Soldatenleben</i> (Athenäum, <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>))</li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Paget, Baron Reginald Thomas (1951). <i>Manstein: His Campaigns and His Trial</i>. London: Collins. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/5582465" rel="nofollow">5582465</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AErich+von+Manstein&amp;rft.aufirst=Baron+Reginald+Thomas&amp;rft.aulast=Paget&amp;rft.btitle=Manstein%3A+His+Campaigns+and+His+Trial&amp;rft.date=1951&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F5582465&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pub=Collins&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Stahlberg, Alexander (1990). <i>Bounden Duty: The Memoirs of a German Officer, 1932–1945</i>. London: Brassey's. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3-548-33129-7" title="Special:BookSources/3-548-33129-7">3-548-33129-7</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AErich+von+Manstein&amp;rft.aufirst=Alexander&amp;rft.aulast=Stahlberg&amp;rft.btitle=Bounden+Duty%3A+The+Memoirs+of+a+German+Officer%2C+1932%931945&amp;rft.date=1990&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=3-548-33129-7&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pub=Brassey%27s&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Stein, Marcel (2007). <i>Field Marshal Von Manstein, a Portrait: The Janus Head</i>. Solihill, West Midlands: Helion and Company. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-906033-02-1" title="Special:BookSources/1-906033-02-1">1-906033-02-1</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AErich+von+Manstein&amp;rft.aufirst=Marcel&amp;rft.aulast=Stein&amp;rft.btitle=Field+Marshal+Von+Manstein%2C+a+Portrait%3A+The+Janus+Head&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1-906033-02-1&amp;rft.place=Solihill%2C+West+Midlands&amp;rft.pub=Helion+and+Company&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
